10/23/19, 10:37 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: I will now outline some resources we can use for learning.

On a desktop, I will recommend using the *jupyter notebook*

To get it, download and install Anaconda from this link
https://www.anaconda.com/distribution/

At installation, remember to check the box asking to add it to _PATH_ or _environmental variable_
10/23/19, 10:40 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: If you have a low memory PC, don't bother installing Anaconda. It might slow things down for you.

You can install jupyter notebook and it's dependencies by opening your command prompt and typing

_pip install jupyter notebook_

I'm presuming you already have python installed and have an internet connection.
10/23/19, 10:44 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: To launch the notebook, create a new folder somewhere on your desktop, and open it up.

Hold down the shift key and right click on a blank area (making sure nothing is selected if the folder has any contents), select _open command prompt (or powershell) here_ depending on the version of windows you are using.


This opens a command prompt in that very directory/folder/location.

Type:
_jupyter notebook_

Hit enter. The notebook fires up and opens on a browser.

If you are on a Mac or Linux please indicate later if you will need some guidance.
10/23/19, 10:44 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: If you can reach a browser but cannot afford to install packages, the site pythonanywhere.com has resources for you.
10/23/19, 10:45 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: If you are on a mobile, get the app _SoloLearn_.

The _Code playground_ can be used to write and save code.
10/23/19, 10:47 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Finally, one of the best _free_ resource I have come across for learning this language is w3schools.com/python

10/23/19, 10:57 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Web development can be complicated sometimes and many people misunderstand what is required. That said, there are two aspects of web development: the front end and the backend.

The front end is where the items on _1_ in your list come in. JavaScript has frameworks for the back two. Node.js being the most popular.

However, the backend is where the logic is the site resides. This is where python frameworks are a great asset. These include Django and Flask amongst others.

For the web with python, after learning the basics, you will need to zero in on a web framework so you can develop the backend with it. When done, with basic knowledge of HTML and CSS, you get string a front end to it and have a full site from static to full fledged web apps like e-commerce and game sites.
10/23/19, 10:58 PM - +234 903 182 7815: You can use Python for back end web development. Python web dev frameworks include: Django, Flask and Falcon.

10/24/19, 8:18 AM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *#PythonTip_byFesh*

Python is object oriented and almost everything in the language is an object. This is a very important aspect that determines a lot of things.

Every object has its ```properties``` and ```methods``` .

To work with each, one need know what properties they posses and what ```method``` they respond to.

To find the type of an object do:
_type(object)_

Eg:
aList = [1, 2, "Fred"]
aTuple = (1, 2, "Fred") 

aList is a python list and possess the properties as well as responds to the methods of a list.

 ```Properties``` : mutable, iterable.
 ```Methods``` : append(), pop(), index(), sort() etc

_aTuple_ is a Tuple.
Essentially a python list but different in that it's ```immutable``` : cannot be changed once created. Responds to two methods _index()_  and _count()_. Being immutable they cannot be sorted, appended to, members popped etc.

The object type determines what properties it possess and what methods it responds to.
10/24/19, 8:30 AM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *Teaser*: Find the basic data types in python, their properties and the methods they respond to.

Google is your friend.

10/26/19, 12:39 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *My Review: Pydroid and SoloLearn*

Pydroid is beautiful! 

It has as good an IDE as SoloLearn's Code Playground but better interpreter. It's able to take user input one by one unlike the Code Playground. More so, it has recourse for install and loading modules. Integrates with Pastebin. For other languages, the owners have other app offering. 

SoloLearn has a full feature learning resource/course and challenge system for practise as well as a large repo of scripts from its user community which includes several other programming and markup languages. 

*Recommendation*
_Running code_ == pydroid
_Learning_ == SoloLearn.

10/27/19, 7:56 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Any case/scenario where some form of hierarchy is needed in normal or reverse order _sorted()_ will be very handy.

In a data set for analysis, a dictionary holding values user data might need to be sorted alphabetically in ascending or descending order for uniformity and some order.

A dictionary of English words (words for keys and values for meaning) will make more sense sorted alphabetically.

In a webapp where an API call returns a series of JSON query, it might be needful to know which response was received before the order to determine which transaction happened first, or which transactions failed or succeeded.

There are several scenario but these come to my mind now.

10/28/19, 9:49 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Yes you can.
Matter of fact, sometimes recruiters mixed both up.
What is always obtainable is that, to work in an area, apart from being able to manipulate data, you should have a fair amount of knowledge of the operations of that industry. For eg, to work in the banking industry, a knowledge of financial things will always make things easier for you if you take up any of these roles with them. Though you can work with data, you need to still understand what variables are important in determining a possible default in giving a person a loan. 10/30/19, 7:54 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Like the name says, it gets the difference between the two sets.

But unlike _difference_ which returns the difference without touching the original set, it *updates* the affected set and alters it's contents.

So _difference_update_ is an *inplace* operation ie changes/alters the contents of original variable.

11/2/19, 2:36 PM - +234 812 053 1560: @2349081987335 I found a quick fix for this which worked perfectly. 

I went to the jupyter_notebook_config.py file and added the code under line 267 to automate opening with chrome whenever I call jupyter notebook in powershell. SEE CODE TO ADD BELOW:

import webbrowser
webbrowser.register('chrome', None, webbrowser.GenericBrowser(r'C:\Program Files (x86)\Google\Chrome\Application\chrome.exe'))
c.NotebookApp.browser = 'chrome'

11/2/19, 9:54 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *Python Variables*
11/2/19, 9:55 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: In python, the single equal sign _=_ is used for assignment. So to create a variable, you basically choose a name and assign some object to it. For eg:

x = "Goat"
11/2/19, 9:55 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: So, x is a variable. It holds the string _Goat_.
11/2/19, 9:55 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: A very important aspect of the namespace in python is this: walk with me....
11/2/19, 9:56 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: x is not actually equal to Goat. Goat is something in the namespace and x points to it. Remember this. It will mean a lot down the line.
11/2/19, 9:57 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: It might not make any sense at the moment but you will remember I said it.
11/2/19, 10:00 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: That said, Python Variables have some recommendations for choosing them.
0. Start all variables with a lower an alphabet not a number. 
1. Variables are required commended to start with a lower case letter.
2. Variables must be a continuous word, no spaces in between.
3. If you will want to use two word use either of 2 conventions:
- join them with an underscore (not a hyphen) _one_list_
or
- write them in camelcase _oneList_
11/2/19, 10:01 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Some names are reserved in python. They include words like _if, in else, elif, for, while, try, except, in, not_ etc. These words are usually highlighted in green by different IDEs.
11/2/19, 10:02 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: They mean particular things in python and _must_ be used for those things alone. We will see them down the line.
11/2/19, 10:03 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: This will do for variables so far.
Summary:
Make a variable by assignment and assignments in python are done with a single equal sign.
11/2/19, 10:04 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *Python (Built-in) Data Types*
11/2/19, 10:05 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: If you are going to do well with python, you must know what type a variable/data is so you will know what properties it has and what methods it responds to
11/2/19, 10:06 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Unlike, many languages, Python is dynamically typed.
11/2/19, 10:08 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: This means that, objects in python have explicit types. This is part of why python is easy to write (but a little drag as runtime needs to determine types and so execute a bit slower) Most languages declare what type an object is on creation.

However, since version 3.4 or there about, Python introduced static typing which can be used on demand.

Don't bother about this if it makes no sense now. It will down the line.
11/2/19, 10:08 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: At this stage, for beginners we are going to concern ourselves with the following data types:
11/2/19, 10:09 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: <Media omitted>
11/2/19, 10:09 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Simply put:
_Python string_ type are everyday alphabets a-zA-Z ie capitals or small. This includes some signs.
11/2/19, 10:11 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: They are written with single or double quotes as a rule. Remember the _x_ variable above that held the string "Goat". Now you know why it is enclosed in quotes.

All strings come with quotes.

Not that x is a variable so can be written without a quote and it will refer to the variable x. However, the variable holds the string _goat_ which must be in quotes.
11/2/19, 10:13 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: The second type is _nunbers_ and pythomg numbers have two types I want us to concern ourselves with:
- _integers_ ie whole numbers: 1,2,33,56,7 etc.
- _floats_ ie numbers with decimal place: 1.0, 4.8, 6.01 etc.
11/2/19, 10:13 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Forget about complex numbers for now. You'll hardly need them
11/2/19, 10:14 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: We will also need to know the _Boolean_ type. This has just two values: true or false.
11/2/19, 10:16 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: To find a python type, use the _type()_ method. Pass the object in the bracket. 

To find the type of our variable x from above, do:

_type(x)_

This will return _class str_ which is a string.
11/2/19, 10:17 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: We'll stop here.

11/2/19, 10:18 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Correct.

It's always a capital T and F. Your IDE will show it in a different color.

11/4/19, 6:25 AM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: @2348036044312
Reading through it I can't find any error. I've not tried to run it for obvious reason. The rest of the code is not available and it would depend on it.

However in I'll point out some things:

When getting things from a form filled in the frontend, unless you are dead sure you have a solid form that can never go wrong, you might wanna get your POST information with a _try/except_ just to be on a safe side since I didn't see you check if the form is valid first. You don't want things breaking there (or anywhere sha) 

1. With this sign up form, you kind of reinvented the wheel. Part of the beauty of Django is that it comes with _batteries_. A builtin Django form would have helped here. There's one at _django.contrib.auth.forms_ you could import the some forms.

That said, it looks like a part of tutorial and the flow of a Django signup is being explained: get POST data from the front, check if email is in use, check if passwords correspond, check if username exists, if it does not, create it, then redirect them to homepage.

11/4/19, 5:29 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: I have gone into the python documentation.

It appears _comprehensions_ like this are actually python _slice()_ function/method under the hood.

_under the hood_ are my own words here. Lol.

As per doc.python.org, these notations are abridgements of the method _slice()_. However, they come with a syntactic flavor of dropping the _slice_ and skipping the intermediate step where you initialize the slice before calling it on an array.

There are also extended Slicing like I used in the Teaser.

Line3: we mutate the variable from line1 taking only the first two elements. So we have [1,3].
With [::-1] at print, it is reversed to [3,1]

11/11/19, 3:59 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: _ind_ is running your index of 1-n and _value_ is taking the members of the _iterable_ names _listOfTable_ which appears to be made of of integers. If it is true that it is made up of integers, then that explains the type error you are getting as you are trying to subscribe to an integer. So you yet a TypeError. You can only subscribe to an array.

When you says (on line 4 where the error is)
_next_val = value[next_value]_, for the first iteration what is happening is something like this:

_next_value = 5[1]_

5 is an integer and you can't subscribe/index into it. TypeError occurs.

11/12/19, 11:21 AM - +234 803 604 4312: Morning guys sorry I was missed the help task yesterday.... was a long day..... glad you finally figured it out.... but likewise, it‚Äôs good to know what was wrong in the code if noticed.... looking at ‚ÄúIn 53‚Äù : using the enumerator method in your for loop... 
the position of the ‚Äúind‚Äù is the loop using the enumerator method automatically counts your iteration... hence there was no need for the ‚Äúind + 1‚Äù the program would do automatic increments and count for you using the enumerator method.

11/12/19, 12:07 PM - +234 816 398 2854: wow, how does this work?
11/12/19, 12:08 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: He's found a working way out. üëç
11/12/19, 12:15 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: If you have a function you want to execute on a collection, use _map()_.

In the example, we have a list of figures each corresponding to the radius of a circle and we have a function _area_circle_ that calculated the circumference of a circle.

Instead of using a for loop to call the function on each of the members, map can takes the function and the list as arguments and perform as the function on each member of the list.

The result is a map object (saving memory) which can be unpacked with the _list()_ method.

I printed the map object first and then unpacked it with _list()_

You are mixing up indentation. Python is sensitive to whitespace at the start of lines.
11/19/19, 9:53 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: That said
11/19/19, 9:59 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: The first four lines are a _function_. The first line of every function ends with a colon so all the remainder of the function should have a consistent indent (whitespace) at their beginning. Choose a number and stick to it. The standard practice is 4 spaces or just use your tab key on the keyboard.

Once the indentation is added, skip a line for some semantic touch/readability so it becomes obvious that the function has ended, then finish up the rest of the lines starting them from the beginning, ie, giving no space at the beginning as they are not part of the _function_.

11/19/19, 10:28 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: The correct answer here is _d_

Partition takes at least one argument. What it does is that it takes a variable produces a 3 item tuple by splits it into three parts.

_index 0_ is the first part, and comprises everything before the argument in the _partition(arg)_.
_index 1_ the argument in the _partition(arg)_
_index 2_ is everything after the argument in _partition(arg)_

In the exercise, _aPoint_ is the argument which is the 3rd element of the variable _test_ ie 'architect'.

When you partition test at _architects_, you have:
_index 0_ as *we are the*
_index 1_ as *architects*
_index 2_ as *of our life*

Combined with regex, the partition method will do wonders for you in NLP.

11/19/19, 10:31 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *Typos corrected + some additions.*

The correct answer here is _d_

Partition takes at least one argument. What it does is that it takes a variable and produces a 3-item tuple by splits it into three parts (indexex/indices).

_index 0_ is the first part, and comprises everything before the argument in the _partition(arg)_.
_index 1_ the argument in the _partition(arg)_
_index 2_ is everything after the argument in _partition(arg)_

In the exercise, _aPoint_ is the argument which is the 3rd element of the _split()_ of the variable _test_ which is 'architect'.

When you partition test at _architects_, you have:
_index 0_ as *we are the*
_index 1_ as *architects*
_index 2_ as *of our life*

Combined with regex, the partition method will do wonders for you in NLP.

11/20/19, 4:20 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *Tip for the day*
A good coding question comprises of three parts:

0. What you are trying to achieve.
1. What you have done in attempt to get this done.
2. What error you are encountering (if any) or what you are you getting that is not what you wanted/expected.

1 and 2 will usually involve a screenshot of the code and result. 

These will assist a helper towards getting a definite solution(s) for you.

11/20/19, 10:26 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: I'm assuming the range you put in is an attempt to make the user not exceed first inputs.

I have used counter to take care of that.

The flow is, ask for an input. Provided it is not _exit_, add it to the list of movies, increment _counter_ by one and keep doing so till it gets to 5 at which point you print the movies entered so far and break out of the loop. But if it is _exit_ that is entered, print the movies you have and then break out of the _while_ loop.

11/20/19, 10:28 PM - +234 705 579 9202: Awesome stuff, 
Can u copy the code here,  so i can paste it in m pydroid and see the output,  instead of typing it again
11/20/19, 10:29 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: movies = []
counter = 0
a = input("Enter movie name: ")

while a.lower() != 'exit':
	movies.append(a)
	counter += 1
	if counter == 5:
		print(movies)
		break
	else:
		a = input("Enter movie name: ")
else:
	print(movies)

11/21/19, 6:22 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: If you are going to do this, you will need your own laptop for learning and practice. 

To be ready, you have to install python on your computer and the recommended IDE for us is the jupyter notebook.

To make things easier, I will ask that you download and install Anaconda on your PC. It comes with python and the jupyter notebook. 

Get it here:
https://www.anaconda.com/distribution/

Get latest version (3.7 at the moment). 
There are 32 and 64 bit versions. Get the one that corresponds with your OS version. If in doubt, get 32bit.

At installation, *remember* to add conda to *PATH*. Check that button. This is critical. It will throw a red flag but don't mind it, continue.

To test that you installed it correctly, open a command prompt and type _python_ and hit enter. If you get no error, you are good to go. If you do, you didn't check to add it to PATH. Uninstall and install again. Check the button to add to PATH. 

PS: Anaconda is large. You will need at least 500MB or 0.5GB to get it.

11/22/19, 12:10 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: I don't know if there are any Django developers in here but I have a tip for them for the day.
11/22/19, 12:32 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *Tip of the Day*
Django humanize

The python web framework that comes with batteries packed, Django, has a set of template filters useful for adding a ‚Äúhuman touch‚Äù to data.

To activate them, add 'django.contrib.humanize' to your INSTALLED_APPS setting. Once done, use {% load humanize %} in templates you want to apply them and you‚Äôll have access to the following filters:

_apnumber - (returns 1-9 spelled out otherwise returns the number) - 1 becomes one and 10 becomes 10._
_intcomma - 4500 becomes 4,500 (for ints, floats or their string format)_.
_intword - 1000000 becomes 1.0 million._
_naturalday - 21 Nov 2019 becomes yesterday. (Going by today's date)_
_naturaltime - 22 Nov 2019 12:22:30 becomes now. (Going by actual time I typed this)_
_ordinal - 1 becomes 1st._

Example applications. I wanted commas in the price total of my products so it went from
{{ order.shipping_cost }} which returned returns/displays 1500.00
to
{{ order.shipping_cost|intcomma }} which returns 1,500.00

These can be very handy for django developers.

11/22/19, 9:28 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Summary:
SQL is the structured query language for database creation and manipulation.

Pandas is a Python library that deals with dataframes. Dataframes are tables and databases are essentially tables too.

The advantage of pandas would be ease of learning curve compared to SQL that can get complex real fast. Moreso, pandas can extract, transform and load (ETL) data. Python can query SQL databases (see Sqlalchemy etc) too. SQL can't touch python, well mostly. 

For purely database applications, SQL will be way more versatile as that is what it lives for. It can also do some analytics if the user has advanced knowledge.

11/26/19, 3:22 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: This is partly correct.
True is any value that is not the numerical 0, the string _None_ and of course _False_.

Anything that is not any of the above 3 evaluates to _False_/zero.

Any other, even if it is a negative number like -8 or 0.0004 is _positive/True_.
11/26/19, 3:26 PM - +234 818 559 0066: Ohhhhh wow, didn‚Äôt know those values also evaluate to True. Thanks for that insight

11/26/19, 4:35 PM - +234 812 053 1560: Further to what my oga have said. If I may explain it a little further from my understanding.
What is going on is adding 2 numpy arrays together.
In this type of addition, the output is simply the sum of corresponding elements. Notice how the middle elements in each array (4) and (1) add to 5
In Python, True is numerically equal to 1, and False is equal to 0. So if you check the math, it all makes sense.

11/27/19, 1:56 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *Tip/Secret for the Day*
medium.com is a site for many wonderdul articles about almost anything.

You can find articles on code solutions for data science and algorithms to data structures and coding challenges/test.

The drag? You can only read so much article in a month (3 or so) for free before coming across the *You read a lot* message and inviting you to pay for unlimited access to articles.

Get over this by either of 2 ways:
- _disable cookies on your browser_.
- _Open the article in your browser's incognito mode_

Read to your fill. üòé

PS: Disabling cookies might have undesirable effects on the rest of your browsing experience so option 2 is more elegant.

11/27/19, 8:48 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: The _call()_ method take one argument. It evaluates if it has a positive value and prints true if it does and false if not.

call(1-1) is same as call(0).
Since 0 is not positive (remember 0, None and False are _valueless_ entities), it returns *false*.

On call(1-2) which is call(-1), the argument has a positive value since it is  not any of the 3 non-values. So the function evaluate to true.

12/11/19, 6:30 PM - +234 812 053 1560: #import regex module
import re

def passwordIsOk():
    """
    passwordIsOk accepts a string password input and will return true or 
    false if the below certain conditions
    
    """
    while True:
        password = input("Enter a password: ")
        if len(password) < 10: #inputed password must be at least 10 char str
            print("Password must have at least 10 characters")
        elif re.search('[0-9]', password) is None:
            print("Password must contain a number")
        elif re.search('[A-Z]', password) is None:
            print("Password must have at least a capital letter")
        elif re.search('[$, #, %, &, *]', password) is None:
            print("Password must contain one special character $, #, %, & or *") #searches if any of the listed special characters is entered
        elif re.search('[\s]', password): #searches for any white spaces entered in password
            print("Password cannot contain a white space")

        else:
            print("Your password meets the requirement proceed")
            break
passwordIsOk()

12/23/19, 8:51 AM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: The issue of working from and on the desired folder is always a very sticky issue if you are not sure.
12/23/19, 8:56 AM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: The best way to get round this tricky issue at least initially till you learn how to reference a desired folder (something we might wanna look at at a point) is to do the following:
0. Have the files you want to use in a particular folder.
1. While inside this folder in your windows explorer, make sure no file is selected, hold down the shift key and right-click in a blank portion of the screen, select _Open Powershell window here_ (below Windows 7 will show command prompt. It's still ok).

This opens a powershell window or command prompt in that very folder. 

Start your jupyter notebook and your file will be directly in your path. To refer to it just use its name and extension and it will locate it.

*Otherwise*
To open a file not directly in your path, you need to explicitly write/declare the full path.
12/23/19, 8:59 AM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: We need the type of error here.

It is usually on the very last line of the error message.

Two things could cause this. If you are trying to read a file, try reading it with another file format instead of the utf-8. Try using latin-1.

Eg
open(filename, encoding = 'latin-1')

There are a number of encoding formats. If latin1 doesn't work some other might work still. UTF-8 is the most widely used that supports the standard set of special characters.

12/23/19, 4:39 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: Yes.
You will end up with a list initial but as you iterate over it it returns a string on the fly that can be spilt.

Eg:
#Convert col to list
to_split = df.colName.tolist()

#Use ternary state to generate list lats and lons
lon = [line.split(',')[0] for line in to_split]

lat = [line.split(',')[1] for line in to_split]

12/23/19, 5:13 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *Tip for the Day*
Some basic statistical operations on pandas dataframes columns (aka Series). 

*_mean_*
df.col.mean()

*_maximum_*
df.col.max()

*_minimum_*
df.col.min()

*_sum/total_*
df.col.sum()

PS: the column must be of dtype int or float (ie, a number) to work.

12/25/19, 9:28 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: With tools like PowerBI, Qlik, Tableau and similar solutions, you have a set of functionality built in.

One of the biggest challenge with them is their lack of ability to collect _wild data_. By wild I mean data in unconventional places: web scrapping, APIs, etc.

With them, more often than not, you need an already structured data usually produced inside organisations. Organizations have a standard data management system so their data is mostly in a particular structure: sales data is in the form of name, address, product, price, etc. Almost static. Their clear advantage is ability to produce more beautiful visualization. However, a pretty dashboard with poor info is still useless. 

Move over to data you need to catch in the wild: an API, on the open internet, no particular structure, whatsapp messages for eg, stock market price off a wallstreet website etc. You need coding tools to catch these. After getting them, they are usually in very bad structure which needs to be cleaned and maybe transformed into some other format and loaded into a system like the above mentioned ones. This is the whole notion about data engineering and ETL (Extract, Transform, Load). Pandas is critical in ETL as well as in the actual analysis of data.

These are some few technical differences.

Tools like Power BI are upping the ante in their ability to get data from the _wild_ by adding access to traditional sources and some APIs. However, it is still largely under equipped beyond analysis semi structured data.

12/28/19, 6:55 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *Tip of the Day*

What to do when you are stuck with a python method. 

What have you been doing:

Google it and check out a similar issue and it's solution on stackoverflow and/or other developer forums? 

Ok, you are more of the guy who wants to get it and you click the 'videos' tab of your Google search? Nice! That's even better. 

But you are missing the best way to solve your problems: the official documentation. 

Each solution you read up or watch is a flavor of the documentation that the author fancies or that did it for their very scenario. However, the docs will tell you all there is to it with examples.

Next time you get stuck, think official documentation. This is the explanation with the simplest examples by the developers of the package you see working with.

The python _help()_ method might be confusing if one is not at least an intermediate user.

Even a third option is some IDEs like VSCode that will give you hints on methods. Again for a beginner, it can get tricky real quick.

The official web documentation is always the friendliest.

12/30/19, 8:16 AM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: They both appear as the same thing when printed as you might have seen.

This is why it is important to know the type one is working with in python.

The difference arises from the type which in turn lends them to different properties and response to different methods.

Since one is a Series object, it is a single col and cannot accommodate another col. On the other hand the other is a DataFrame object and can take more cols, etc.
12/30/19, 9:46 AM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: *Tip of the day*

What's your Proficiency?

If you are learning a programming language like python (or any skill at all), at a point you will wonder how much of it you have grasped and whether you are set to fit a role requiring it. You are in the right track if you have. If you have not, think about. 

I've had a couple of us here ask me this (privately) on the same day. Before that, someone not in the group sent a voicenote detailing same concern: generally, how do you figure out where you are? 

I have two suggestions at the moment if you are here:
 
0. Take a Pluralsight (or similar) proficiency test. This grades you as Novice, Proficient or Expert. The part I like is that it references the areas you have a gap. I don't however like the extent of the technicality of the questions vs time allotted.

1. Projects: no matter how small, begin to work on projects. Build a mimic of something, participate in one, submitting real code.

Unlike the first which forces answers from you (and so errors), the second makes you apply what you know and find solutions to what you don't to make things work. This is the heart of a developer's mindset. Nobody expects you to know it all, but you must know what's involved and how to find it.

If you are on a Windows 10 you can't really turn off Windows update. Not easily/convenient anyway. And yes, I think it sucks that MS thought that's a great move.

To change this windows behavior, you will need to adjust the registry settings of Windows update.
12/30/19, 9:11 PM - +234 903 138 1743: Please can you take us through on it,  I also face the same issue
12/30/19, 9:31 PM - Ejike Okonkwo Acipite Bro: Yes, am using Windows 10.
12/30/19, 9:31 PM - Paschal Chukwuemeka Amah: PS:
_Changing things in this part of your Windows machine without knowing what they entail could make a lot of things go wrong._ üòÅ

Don't say Fesh said. However I'm sure on this one if do exactly as I have outlined. Don't get too smart, abeg (ie please)

On your keyboard, hold the windows sign and press _R_.

Type into the dialog box that comes up _services.msc_ and hit enter.

In the resulting window (showing all services running or not), find _Windows Update_, right-click on it and click on _properties_.

In the dialog box that comes up, find the _Startup type_, click on it and select _Disabled_ from the dropdown menu. Click _apply_ and then _ok_. *Restart your computer*

You should be clear.
